 S. Chauhan, J. This appeal has been preferred against the impugned judgment and order dated 30.5.2007, passed by Special Judge of the Designated Court under the Terrorist and Disruptive Activities (Prevention) Act, 1987 (hereinafter referred to as the TADA) for the Bombay Blast, Greater Bombay, in the Bombay Blast Case	Issue
No. 1/1993, convicting the Appellant u s 5 TADA, and awarding the punishment of 5 years RI, alongwith a fine of Rs. 25,000/, and in default of payment of fine, to further undergo RI for 6 months.	Issue
Facts and circumstances giving rise to this appeal are that As the facts of this case and all legal issues involved herein have been elaborately dealt with in the connected appeal i.e. Criminal Appeal No. 1728 of 2007 Yakub Abdul Razak Memon v. State of Maharashtra thr. CBI, it may be pertinent to mention only the relevant facts and charges against the Appellant (A20).	None
Bombay Blast took place on 12.3.1993 in which 257 persons lost their lives and 713 were injured.	None
In addition thereto there had been loss of property worth several crores.	Facts
The Bombay police investigated the matter at initial stage but subsequently it was entrusted to the Central Bureau of Investigation (hereinafter referred to as CBI) and on conclusion of the investigation, a chargesheet was filed against a large number of accused persons.	Facts
Out of the accused persons against whom chargesheet was filed, 40 accused could not be put to trial as they have been absconding.	Facts
Thus, the Designated Court under TADA framed charges against 138 accused persons.	Facts
During the trial, 11 accused died and 2 accused turned hostile.	Facts
Further the Designated Court discharged 2 accused during trial and the remaining persons including Appellant (A20) stood convicted.	Facts
The Appellant had been charged for general conspiracy which is framed against all the accused persons for the offences punishable u s 3(3) TADA and Section 120B of Indian Penal Code, 1860 (hereinafter referred to as Indian Penal Code) read with Sections 3(2)(i)(ii), 3(3), (4), 5 and 6 TADA and read with Sections 302, 307, 326, 324, 427, 435, 436, 201 and 212 Indian Penal Code and offences Under Sections 3 and 7 read with Sections 25(IA), (IB) (a) of the Arms Act, 1959 (hereinafter referred to as the Arms Act), Sections 9B(1)(a)(b)(c) of the Explosives Act, 1884.	Facts
Sections 3, 4(a)(b), 5 and 6 of the Explosive Substances Act, 1908 and Section 4 of the Prevention of Damage to Public Property Act, 1984.	None
In addition to the general charge of conspiracy, he had also been charged u s 3(3) TADA, u s 5 TADA for keeping one AK56 rifle plus two empty magazines and committed an offence in respect of the same u s 6 TADA, and u s 3(4) TADA read with Section 212 Indian Penal Code, for harbouring criminals.	Facts
After conclusion of the trial, the Appellant (A20) had been convicted u s 5 TADA, and awarded the sentence as mentioned hereinabove.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Shri Sunil Kumar, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the Appellant (A20), has submitted that conviction of the Appellant (A20) u s 5 TADA, was not warranted in view of the fact that the recovery had not been proved in accordance with law.	Arguments of Petitioner
The disclosure statement alleged to have been made under the provision of Section 27 of the Indian Evidence Act, 1872 (hereinafter called Evidence Act) has not been strictly followed.	Arguments of Petitioner
The said alleged disclosure statement did not bear the signature of the Appellant (A20).	Arguments of Petitioner
There were two panch witnesses, only one has been examined.	Arguments of Petitioner
The panch witness examined in the case had been a stock witness in the police as he had appeared as a panch witness in other cases.	Arguments of Petitioner
He was the resident of an area in close vicinity of the office of the Crime Branch of the police department.	Arguments of Petitioner
The watchman of the Ghanshyam building from which the recovery had been made, has not been examined.	Arguments of Petitioner
None of the neighbours of that building have been examined as witnesses.	Arguments of Petitioner
There are material contradictions omissions in the deposition of the witnesses.	Arguments of Petitioner
Even the case number has wrongly been mentioned.	Arguments of Petitioner
The L.A.C. No. 22/93 had been manipulated and it was shown as L.A.C. No. 23/93.	Arguments of Petitioner
Even the recovery of the AK56 rifle does not give rise to the presumption of possession.	Arguments of Petitioner
It was further submitted that the confession of the Appellant (A20) had been obtained by coercion by severely beating him.	Arguments of Petitioner
Therefore, the appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
5.	None
On the contrary, Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the Respondent, has vehemently opposed the appeal contending that the confession of the Appellant (A20) has not been relied upon by the Designated Court, therefore, it remains inconsequential.	Arguments of Respondent
Only one of the panch witnesses had been examined in order to avoid multiplicity.	Arguments of Respondent
Mere nonexamination of the other panch witnesses would not make the case of the prosecution doubtful.	Arguments of Respondent
The recovery had been made strictly in accordance with law and in case the recovery had been made at the instance of the disclosure statement and from the place on which the Appellant (A20) had control over, the presumption has rightly been drawn, particularly in view of the fact that the Appellant (A20) did not lead any evidence in defence.	Arguments of Respondent
Thus, the appeal is liable to be dismissed.	Arguments of Respondent
We have considered the rival submissions made by learned Counsel for the parties and perused the record.	Reasoning
Evidence against the Appellant (A20) Confessional statement of the Appellant (A20) Confessional statement of Murad Ibrahim Khan (A130) Deposition of Mohamed Ayub Mohamed Umar (PW72) Deposition of Sahadev (PW181) Deposition of Nagesh Shivdas Lohar (PW356) Deposition of Shivaji Shankar Sawant (PW524) Recovery of AK56 rifle and two magazines Confessional statement of the Appellant (A20)	None
The confessional statement made by the Appellant has not been relied upon by learned Special Judge.	Reasoning
Therefore, we are not making any reference to it and it has to be ignored.	Reasoning
More so, we do not find any force in the submission made by Shri Sunil Kumar that there had been two FIRs in respect of the same incident as a large number of remand applications had been filed and it is evident from the application that at initial stage it was shown as L.A.C. 23/93, but a correction had been made though without initials by the person who made the correction, but in his subsequent application it had been shown as L.A.C. 22/93.	Reasoning
More so, the FIR number connecting this case is the same.	Reasoning
Only one FIR had been exhibited in the court as Exhibit 1284A dated 18.4.1993 and it contains case No. L.A.C. 23/93.	Reasoning
Therefore, we do not think that the submission requires further consideration.	Reasoning
Confessional statement of Murad Ibrahim Khan (A130) He had disclosed that he was fully acquainted with Yakub Yeda and the Appellant (A20).	None
Thus, he was having acquaintance with hardened criminals.	Reasoning
Deposition of Mohamed Ayub Mohamed Umar (PW72)	Reasoning
He is a panch witness and a hawker, selling fruits on the footpath near Crawford Market.	Reasoning
He deposed that on 17.4.1993 at about 12.45 hours, he was called to the police station by a Hawaldar and there Constable, Shivaji Sawant, P.I. asked him whether he would like to act as a panch witness in a case related to the Bombay Blast.	Reasoning
He consented for the same and one more panch witness was also present in the said room.	Reasoning
In his presence, accused (A20) disclosed his name as Salim Khan and he said in Hindi that he was in possession of one AK56 rifle and two magazines in his workshop the recovery of which he would get effected.	Reasoning
The witness further corroborated the entire version of the recovery.	Reasoning
The witness further deposed that the police party alongwith the accused (A20) and the panch witnesses proceeded in the police vehicle and towards the place disclosed by the Appellant (A20) and entered the building pointed out by the Appellant (A20).	Reasoning
The said workshop had a loft and one staircase.	Reasoning
One police officer went on the loft.	Reasoning
Thereafter, all other persons followed him.	Reasoning
On the directions of the Appellant, cartons, scrap material and gunny bag were found.	Reasoning
The said gunny bag was turned out for emptying the same.	Reasoning
One AK56 rifle and two magazines were taken out of the said gunny bag.	Reasoning
The AK56 rifle and magazines were separately wrapped in brown paper and three packets were prepared and sealed separately.	Reasoning
The packets were signed by him, copanchas and Shri Sawant.	Reasoning
In crossexamination, the witness (PW. 2) said that label put up on the recovered goods had his signature and the goods had been seized.	Reasoning
He further denied the suggestion made by the defence that he was the regular panch witness for the police.	Reasoning
However, he had admitted that occasionally, he had worked as such and he had been a panch witness in other cases.	Reasoning
Deposition of Shivaji Shankar Sawant, P.I. (PW524)	Reasoning
He deposed that on 17.4.1993, he was interrogating the Appellant (A20) who was arrested in C.R. No. 71/93 alongwith some other police officials.	Reasoning
During the said interrogation, the Appellant (A20) consented to make the voluntary statement and in the presence of the panch witnesses and other police officials, the Appellant (A20) made a disclosure statement in Hindi.	Reasoning
He recorded the same in the panchanama.	Reasoning
The panchnama was read over to the Appellant (A20) and the panch witnesses.	Reasoning
It was signed by the panch witnesses and countersigned by him (A20).	Reasoning
He disclosed that the Appellant (A20) had consented to show the place and take out AK56 rifle and two empty magazines kept by the Appellant (A20).	Reasoning
He further deposed that panchnama of the recovery from the workshop of the Appellant (A20) was correct.	None
The same bears his signature and the signatures of panch witnesses and it was completed on 18.4.1993.	None
He was a Police Inspector and working as a Dy. S.P. for Protection of Civil Rights, Unit Bombay.	None
He clarified the correction regarding L.A.C. Nos. 22/93 and 23/93 and explained that there was a correction on the L.A.C. numbers.	Reasoning
However, he had admitted that he had made that correction while registering the said case though it did not bear his initials and he was not in a position to give any reason for not putting his initials and correction was necessary as there had been some typographical error.	Reasoning
He further stated that he arrested the accused formally in L.A.C. No. 23/93 at 1.25 a.m. on 18.4.1993.	None
In paras 65 and 66, he deposed that he did not register any L.A.C. No. 22/93 and also did not know the name of officer who had registered L.A.C. 22/93.	None
Deposition of Sahadev (PW181)	None
He deposed that on 14.5.1993, he received requisition from Worli Police station for recording the confessional statement of the Appellant A20 and he had proved the said confessional statement.	Reasoning
Deposition of Nagesh Shivdas Lohar (PW356)	Reasoning
He has deposed that on 17.4.1993, he alongwith Shivaji Sawant and one Head Constable interrogating the Appellant (A20) in case No. C.R. 71/93.	Reasoning
He corroborated that Shri Sawant had asked the Appellant (A20) whether he wanted to make the voluntary statement.	Reasoning
Thereafter, Appellant made the statement in Hindi.	Reasoning
He recorded the statement of the Appellant in Hindi in the panchnama which was marked as Exh. 378 and is the same panchanama recorded by him about the statement of Appellant (A20) between 17th and 18th April, 1993.	Reasoning
The recovery of the AK56 rifle and two magazines had been made on 17th/18th April, 1993 and in respect of the same panchanama Exh. 383	Reasoning
makes it clear that Farid Alam Rais Alam Qureshi and Mohamad Ayub Mohamad Umar had been the panch witnesses and in their presence the Appellant (A20) voluntarily made a disclosure statement that the AK56 rifle was kept in his workshop.	Reasoning
This panchnama was concluded at 23.10 hours on 17.4.1993.	Reasoning
The panchnama has been signed by both the witnesses as well as by the Inspector of Police, Shri S.S. Sawant.	Reasoning
However, it does not bear the signature of the Appellant (A20).	Reasoning
It further shows that in continuation of the same, the search was conducted and it reveals that after making the disclosure statement, the police had taken the accused alongwith the panch witnesses to a closed workshop named as Bona Parte industry belonging to him.	Reasoning
The said workshop was having its shutter down and locked and in absence of the key the police forced open the lock and opened the same.	Reasoning
The panchas alongwith the accused (A20) and the police party entered the workshop and found that there was no electricity however, they found that the loft measured approximately 16 x 40 had a loft with a staircase.	Reasoning
The Appellant (A20) led all of them to the loft by staircase.	Reasoning
On reaching there the Appellant (A20) took out a box and scrap was removed.	Reasoning
One AK56 rifle and empty magazines were found wrapped in gunny sack.	Reasoning
The police examined the said material and prepared the recovery memo.	Reasoning
The recovery memo contained one AK56 assault rifle of folding type butt, in rusted condition but had been greased and two magazines of AK56 assault rifle with no identification marks and numbers and it was in rusted condition and greased.	Reasoning
The Designated Court after appreciating the evidence on record came to the conclusion as under A20 having not explained the reason of his knowledge of such a contraband articles being kept in a said factory of which he was partner the same will lead only to the inference of himself having kept the same.	None
The same is obvious as A20 could have knowledge about the same in three contingencies i.e. a) he had kept the contrabands himself b) having seen somebody keeping the same there c) somebody had told him that the same being kept at the said place or having seen himself of such articles being kept at that place.	None
In view of failure of A20 to give any explanation regarding his knowledge being due to the reasons as stated in the aforesaid clauses b) and c)	None
the same will lead to the conclusion as stated aforesaid.	None
With regard to matter stated in clause c) aforesaid it can be additionally added that since the accused was also partner of the said shop allowing the remaining of such articles at the said place would also attract the liability for the same The same is the case regarding the submission advanced on the basis of A20 at the time of recovery not having the key of the relevant gala.	None
In light of the aforesaid discussion it is difficult to accept the submission canvassed that the evidence only establish the knowledge of A20 of the contraband material lying at the said place and the same does not amount to himself being in conscious possession of the same.	Reasoning
Needless to add that the inferences flowing from the statement made by A20 consciously are not of a nature of denoting himself not being in conscious possession of contraband articles within the notified area We have appreciated the evidence on record and the case depends upon the veracity of evidence regarding recovery.	Reasoning
There is sufficient material to show that the recovery had been made at the behest of the Appellant (A20) from the factory owned by a partnership to which he was the partner alongwith one Surjit Singh.	Reasoning
In such a factsituation, he ought to have explained the reason source of his knowledge of such contraband articles being kept in his factory.	Reasoning
In the instant case, as he has not mentioned that he had seen someone keeping the articles there, or somebody had told him about that, or he had seen the things lying there, the only reasonable inference is drawn that he himself had kept the same at that place.	Reasoning
Being a partner of the firm if he was having the knowledge that some contraband were lying in his premises, he ought to have informed the police if he had no guilty mind.	Reasoning
So far as the explanation that at the time of recovery he did not have the key, would not be enough to tilt the balance in his favour.	Reasoning
The fact that he did not have the key becomes totally redundant, as no conclusion can be drawn that the Appellant (A20) was not in possession of the said premises, and in such a factsituation, it cannot be held that the Appellant (A20) was not in conscious possession of the contraband material.	Reasoning
We do not find any force in the submissions made by Shri Sunil Kumar, learned senior counsel appearing for the Appellant (A20), that the panch witness was the resident of Sitaram building, which was opposite to the office of the Commissioner of Police, or in a very close proximity of the same, and was working on the footpath nearby the said building, and he had acted earlier as a panch witness in test identification parade.	Reasoning
The police when searching for a panch witness, need not go to far off place of the police station as the panchnama is required to be recorded in a close proximity of time, when the accused apprehending his disclosure statement.	Reasoning
Therefore, on such material suspicion about the credential of the police or panch witnesses cannot be doubted, unless there is some material to prove the contrary.	Reasoning
Had it been picked up from a far off place, criticism could have been otherwise as to why the panch witness could not be called from neighbourhood.	Reasoning
The panch witness Mohamed Ayub Mohamed Umar (PW72) could not be held to be a tutored witness or acting at the behest of the prosecution only on the ground that he had also been the witness in another case.	Reasoning
It does not give a reason to draw inference that he was a stock panch witness unless it is shown that he had acted in such capacity in a very large number of cases.	Reasoning
More so, it cannot be held that Mohamed Ayub Mohamed Umar (PW72) was not an independent witness, or acting under the pressure of the police as he was carrying the business illegally without any license.	Reasoning
More so, the Appellant (A20) had made the disclosure statement in his presence, he could explain the same.	Reasoning
Therefore, it could not be held that he was deposing falsely.	Reasoning
We do not see any reason as to why his evidence should not be relied upon.	Reasoning
Minor omissions contradictions regarding labeling and sealing are not really the contradictions which go to the root of the matter.	Reasoning
Nonexamination of the watchman of Ghanshyam Industrial Estate, or omission of factor regarding electricity being not mentioned in the panchnama, or noncollection of broken lock, are the omissions of trivial nature, and do not warrant any undue importance for doubting the evidence of recovery.	Reasoning
Law does not require the witness to corroborate the evidence of an independent witness.	Reasoning
Thus, the evidence of Mohamed Ayub Mohamed Umar (PW72) duly corroborated by the contemporaneous panchnama is trustworthy.	Reasoning
As the Appellant (A20) made a statement leading to the discovery of AK56 assault rifle and two magazines having kept in his workshop and the same had been found concealed on the loft, he cannot escape from the liability of possessing and concealing of the same, thus liable to be punished u s 5 TADA.	Decision
We see no reason to interfere with the conclusion drawn by the learned Designated Court.	Decision
The appeal is accordingly dismissed.	Decision
This appeal has been preferred against the judgments and orders dated 11.10.2006 and 31.5.2007, passed by Special Judge of the Designated Court under the TADA for Bombay Blast, Greater Bombay, in the Bombay Blast Case No. 1/93, by which the Appellant had been found guilty u s 5 TADA and on that count, he was sentenced to suffer RI for 5 years, and ordered to pay a fine of Rs. 25,000/, and in default of payment of fine, to suffer further RI for 6 months.	Issue
He (A21) was further convicted Under Sections 3 and 7 read with Section 25(1A)(1B)(a) of the Arms Act, but no separate sentence was awarded for the same.	Issue
Facts and circumstances giving rise to this appeal are that In addition to the first charge of general conspiracy, he was charged for attending conspiratorial meetings at Dubai where criminal conspiracy was discussed for distributing arms and ammunition to coconspirators and for providing funds to them.	Facts
Thus, he (A21) was charged Under Sections 3(3) and (4) TADA.	Facts
Further, he (A21) was charged with unauthorisedly being in possession of one U.S. Carbine 0.300 with three magazines and 28 cartridges in the notified area under TADA, between January 1993 and 5th April, 1993, and thus, charged Under Sections 5 and 6 TADA, and further under the provisions of Sections 3 and 7 read with Sections 25(A), 25(1B)(a) of the Arms Act.	Facts
After the trial, the Appellant (A21) stood acquitted of all the charges except charges u s 5 TADA and under the Arms Act.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Shri Mushtaq Ahmad, learned Counsel appearing for the Appellant has submitted that the Appellant had wrongly been involved in the offence and convicted, though there is no sufficient evidence on record, to involve the Appellant in the crime.	Arguments of Petitioner
The evidence particularly the confessional statement of the Appellant and the depositions of other witnesses particularly, Bhaskar Babu Rao Jadhav (PW57), Dayandeo Sonaji Geete (PW320), Vijay Meru (PW561), and Shivajirao Kondiram Babar (PW683) etc. are not worth reliance.	Arguments of Petitioner
The confessional statement of the Appellant cannot be relied upon, as it was not made voluntarily and truthfully.	Arguments of Petitioner
The appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
29.	Arguments of Petitioner
Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the State has submitted that the Appellant was a close associate of Tiger Memon (AA).	Arguments of Respondent
He was fully involved in the entire episode including the conspiracy.	Arguments of Respondent
He has wrongly been acquitted for the said charge.	Arguments of Respondent
Thus, no interference is called for.	Arguments of Respondent
The appeal is liable to be dismissed.	Arguments of Respondent
We have heard learned Counsel for the parties and perused the record.	None
Evidence against the Appellant (A21) Confessional statement of the Appellant (A21) Deposition of Bhaskar Babu Rao Jadhav (PW57) Deposition of Dayandeo Sonaji Geete (PW320) Deposition of Vijay Meru (PW561) Deposition of Shivajirao Kondiram Babar (PW683) Confessional Statement of Appellant (A21)	None
His (A21) confessional statement had been recorded, however, the same had been discarded by the Designated Court, thus it cannot be considered.	Reasoning
The evidence against the Appellant (A21) remains the recovery of the aforesaid arms and ammunition.	Reasoning
In fact, an FIR had been registered on 5.4.1993 at 6.10 p.m. that the Appellant (A21) was found in possession of a Carbine of 30 Caliber of U.S. make, three magazines and 28 cartridges without holding a fire arms license.	Reasoning
It was further revealed that on that day, the police got information that the Appellant (A21) who was a resident of Pydhonie, Mumbai was scheduled to come near a mosque opposite Pydhonie Police Station to acquire arms and ammunition of foreign make.	Reasoning
On that information the trap was arranged near the Pydhonie Police Station.	Reasoning
At about 1.30 p.m. the trap party received the predecided signal, on which the Appellant (A21) was searched, however he was not carrying any arms and ammunition with him.	Reasoning
He was brought to the DCB, CID office and during interrogation he expressed his willingness to make a voluntary statement.	Reasoning
Therefore, two panchas were called from the nearby area and in their presence, the Appellant (A21) disclosed that he had acquired two Carbines of foreign make and some arms and ammunition from one Mujahidan and one of the said Carbine had been concealed at Naryalwadi, Mazgaon.	Reasoning
The Appellant (A21) led the police party and Panchas to Naryalwadi Muslim Cemetery (Kabaristan), Mazgaon and from the said graveyard he took out one gunny bag duly tied with a rope.	Reasoning
It had been concealed in thickly grown trees and shrubs.	Reasoning
On being examined, one single barrel Carbine, 28 cartridges and 3 magazines were found.	Reasoning
Panchnamas have made for his disclosure statement as well as for recovery of the said articles.	Reasoning
Deposition of Bhaskar Babu Rao Jadhav (PW57)	Reasoning
He is a panch witness.	Reasoning
He supported the case of the prosecution and proved the disclosure statement of the Appellant (A21) as well as the recovery made at his behest.	Reasoning
He gave a full description of how the Appellant (A21) made the disclosure statement and how the recoveries were made.	Reasoning
It corroborates the version given in the FIR.	Reasoning
However, in his crossexamination, he (PW57) stated that he (PW57) had prepared the notes for deposing in the court.	Reasoning
He had given full details of the incident of 5.4.1993.	Reasoning
In crossexamination he had admitted that he had also acted as a panch witness in 3 more cases.	Reasoning
Deposition of Dayandeo Sonaji Geete (PW320) His deposition revealed that he had accompanied the Appellant (A21) at the time of recovery alongwith others.	None
He admitted in his crossexamination that he himself had not made any entry in the Station Diary regarding the information received by Senior P.I. Shri Kumbhar about the Appellant (A21).	Reasoning
He has further deposed that he could not remember the manner in which the panch witness arrived in the office.	Reasoning
The said panch was brought by the police havaldar.	Reasoning
The Appellant (A21) had been detained in the office of DCB, CID under suspicion due to the receipt of information that he was to be at Pydhonie for acquiring arms.	Reasoning
He also deposed that the office of the Bombay Municipal Corporation was inside the Naryalwadi Kabristan from where the recovery had been made at the instance of the Appellant (A21).	Reasoning
He further proved the recovery and denied the suggestions that the Appellant (A21) did not make any disclosure statement nor any recovery had been made at his behest.	Reasoning
He has also identified Appellant (A21) in the court.	Reasoning
The deposition of Dayandeo Sonaji Geete (PW320) has been fully corroborated by another police official Vijay Meru (PW561) in all respects.	Reasoning
However, he has admitted that he had not made any entry in the Station Diary regarding the information received by Senior P.I. Shri Kumbhar, nor he was aware how the panch witness arrived and who was the police havaldar who brought the panch witnesses.	Reasoning
However, he (PW561) deposed that he had detained the Appellant (A21) on information that he was to be at Pydhonie for acquiring arms.	Reasoning
Appellant (A21) came there at about 1.30 p.m. and on getting the signal, the police party pounced upon him and apprehended him.	Reasoning
Appellant (A21) was searched but he was not carrying any weapon, however, one Ceiko watch, his driving licence, labour card bearing his photograph of Arab Emirates and some Dirhams and a silver ring were found with him.	Reasoning
The inventory of the said articles was prepared and they were seized.	Reasoning
The currency in Dirhams was of the value of Rs. 13 to 14 thousand Indian rupees.	Reasoning
He was interrogated by A.C.P. Shri Babar (PW683) and Senior P.I. Shri Kumbhar.	Reasoning
It was during his interrogation that Appellant (A21) expressed his desire to make the voluntary statement regarding fire arms.	Reasoning
Thus, two panches were called.	Reasoning
The suspect was introduced to the panches.	Reasoning
The memorandum panchnama was prepared in respect of his disclosure statement.	Reasoning
It was signed by panch witnesses and, thus, he supported the recovery of the articles as narrated by the other witnesses.	Reasoning
He had proved the complaint as well as the proforma FIR on the basis of the said complaint.	Reasoning
PW.	Reasoning
561 registered the said offence as LAC No. 18 of 1993 against the Appellant (A21) for the offences punishable Under Sections 3 and 7 read with Section 25 of the Arms Act.	Reasoning
Though he was competent to answer as to whether the panch witnesses had been stock witnesses or whether there was any discrepancy in drawing the memorandum panchnama but defence did not ask any question during his crossexamination.	Reasoning
He (PW561) identified the Appellant (A21) on 24.4.1998 in the court and he has denied the suggestion made by the defence that the Appellant (A21) had not made any disclosure statement, nor the recovery of arms had been made on his disclosure statement.	Reasoning
Deposition of Shivajirao Kondiram Babar, ACP (PW683) In his deposition, he revealed that he had received the information about Appellant (A21) that he would get the weapons near a mosque opposite police station Pydhonie.	Reasoning
He had received the information from a secret source on telephone, he recorded the same and passed on the same to the senior officers.	Reasoning
He deposed that on 5.4.1993 while he was in his office at Crawford Market, he had received the information from his source on telephone to the effect that Appellant (A21) involved in Bombay blast case was in possession of fire arms and would be available at Pydhonie.	Reasoning
Immediately thereafter, he formed a team of police officers and staff and went to the Pydhonie and arrested the Appellant (A21), brought him to the office of DCB, CID at Crawford Market and he (A21) was interrogated.	Reasoning
This witness further supported the prosecution case that he made a desire to make confession voluntarily etc.	Reasoning
The recovered material was sent for FSL, and the report Ext. 1940A was received.	Reasoning
According to which US Carbine gun and 28 cartridges sent for FSL were examined.	Reasoning
The carbine gun was found in working condition, and it was capable of chambering and firing the cartridges recovered in that offence.	Reasoning
So, the report was positive.	None
The Appellant (A21) made a complaint before the Designated Court, and the court directed his medical examination.	Reasoning
He was examined and again sent on police remand.	Reasoning
After appreciating the entire evidence, the learned Designated Court came to the conclusion as under Since criminal conspiracies are hatched in secrecy and it is extremely difficult to collect the direct evidence about the same the established principles of law indicate that the standard of a proof requiring such type of cases is loosened, unnecessary hard standards regarding the identity of a particular person cant be expected Thus, considering in proper perspective all the said evidence, no other conclusion will emerge of A21 having committed all the offences for which he is charged with on the account of commission of overt acts, but the same will lead any prudent man to the legitimate conclusion of A21 having committed the aforesaid acts for the purposes of furthering the object of conspiracy.	Reasoning
In view of above, the Designated Court found him guilty and imposed the punishment as referred to hereinabove.	Decision
We find no reason to interfere with the directions of the Designated Court.	Decision
The appeal is accordingly dismissed.	Decision
This appeal has been preferred against the judgments and orders dated 11.10.2006 and 31.5.2007, passed by the Special Judge of Designated Court under the TADA, in the Bombay Blast Case No. 1 of 1993, by which the Respondents (A20 and A21) had been convicted u s 3(3) TADA, and acquitted of the main charge of conspiracy.	Issue
Facts and circumstances giving rise to this appeal are that In addition to the main charge of general conspiracy, the Respondents were additionally charged u s 3(3) TADA for facilitating the commission of terrorist activities etc., as they had agreed to send persons for receiving training in arms, ammunition and explosives and making arrangements by harbouring and concealing their conspirators terrorists for consideration of money from Tiger Memon (AA).	Facts
They had further been charged with receiving and keeping in possession of one AK56 rifle and two empty magazines with intention to use and commit terrorist acts.	Facts
Further, u s 5 TADA, for possessing the said AK56 rifle u s 6 of TADA, in respect of the same AK56 rifle and lastly, for harbouring and concealing coaccused Javed Chikna, Yakoob Yeda and Ors.	Facts
terrorists coconspirators at Tonk, Rajasthan after the Bombay blast on 12th March, 1993.	Facts
Both the Respondents (A20 and A21) had been found guilty for the offence punishable u s 5 TADA, and awarded the punishment of five years, and ordered to pay a fine of Rs.	Facts
25,000/ and, in default of payment of fine, to suffer further R.I. for six months.	Facts
But acquitted for the charge of conspiracy.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the Appellant, has submitted that in spite of the ample evidence against the said Respondents (A20 and A21) in view of their involvement in conspiracy, the court below committed the grave error in discarding the same.	Arguments of Petitioner
The evidence requires total reappreciation and the confessional statements of the Respondents (A20 and A21) and other coaccused have to be taken into consideration.	Arguments of Petitioner
Therefore, the appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
44.	Arguments of Petitioner
On the other hand, Shri Mushtaq Ahmad, learned Counsel appearing for the Respondents (A20 and A21), has submitted that their confessional statements have rightly been discarded as the same had been recorded in utter disregard to the statutory provisions.	Arguments of Respondent
The appeal lacks merit and is liable to be dismissed.	Decision
We have considered rival submissions made by the counsel for the parties and perused the records.	Reasoning
The confessional statements of Ahmad Shah Khan Salim Durani (A20), Shaikh Aziz Ahmed (A21), Moiddin Abdul Kadar Cheruvattam (A48) and Ismail Abbas Patel (A80) had been relied upon by the prosecution.	Reasoning
The same stood discarded completely by the learned Special Judge on the ground that all the confessional statements had been recorded by the Police officer in utter disregard to the mandatory provisions of Section 15 TADA and Rule 15 of TADA Rules, 1987.	Reasoning
The police officer failed to inform the said accused persons while recording their respective statements that they were not bound to make confessional statement and further failed to warn that, in case, they made statements, the same would be used as evidence against them.	Reasoning
More so, the required certificate was not attached to the said statements.	Reasoning
This Court has laid down parameters for interference against the order of acquittal time and again.	Reasoning
The appellate court should not ordinarily set aside a judgment of acquittal in a case where two views are possible, though the view of the appellate court may be the more probable one.	Reasoning
While dealing with a judgment of acquittal, the appellate court has to consider the entire evidence on record, so as to arrive at a finding as to whether the views of the trial court were perverse or otherwise unsustainable.	Reasoning
The appellate court is entitled to consider whether in arriving at a finding of fact, the trial court had failed to take into consideration admissible evidence and or had taken into consideration the evidence brought on record contrary to law.	Reasoning
Similarly, wrong placing of burden of proof may also be a subjectmatter of scrutiny by the appellate court.	Reasoning
In exceptional cases where there are compelling circumstances, and the judgment under appeal is found to be perverse, the appellate court can interfere with the order of acquittal.	Reasoning
The appellate court should bear in mind the presumption of innocence of the accused and further that the trial courts acquittal bolsters the presumption of his innocence.	Reasoning
Interference in a routine manner where the other view is possible should be avoided, unless there are good reasons for interference.	Reasoning
The findings of fact recorded by a court can be held to be perverse if the findings have been arrived at by ignoring or excluding relevant material or by taking into consideration irrelevant inadmissible material.	Reasoning
The finding may also be said to be perverse if it is against the weight of evidence, or if the finding so outrageously defies logic as to suffer from the vice of irrationality.	Reasoning
In view of the fact that no legal evidence which could be relied upon by the prosecution is available on record, we do not find any fault with the impugned judgment and order.	Decision
The appeal lacks merit and is liable to be dismissed.	Decision
More so, the Respondents (A20 and A21) have filed appeals, as referred to hereinabove, and they have served 4 years of sentence and deposited the fine.	Decision
The appeal stands dismissed.	Decision
Criminal Appeal No. 1311 of 2007 This appeal has been preferred against the judgments and orders dated 11.10.2006 and 31.5.2007, passed by the learned Special Judge of the Designated Court under the TADA, in the Bombay Blast Case No. 1/93.	Issue
The Appellant (A31) was charged with general charge of conspiracy, and in addition thereto, he was further charged for participating in landing and transportation of the arms, ammunition and explosives smuggled into India by the conspirators and coaccused at Shekhadi and further for transporting the RDX explosives in his motor tempo No. MCU 4409, which was part of the said consignment and unloaded the same in the godown of coaccused Liyakat Ali Habib Khan (A85) at MIDC, Thane.	Issue
The Appellant (A31) was convicted u s 3(3) TADA, and sentenced for 5 years rigorous imprisonment, alongwith a fine of Rs. 25,000/, and in default of payment of fine, to suffer further RI for 6 months.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Ms. Farhana Shah, learned Counsel appearing on behalf of the Appellant, has submitted that the Appellant (A31) has been wrongly convicted for the offences under TADA and the Arms Act.	Arguments of Petitioner
The confessional statements of the coaccused could not be relied upon for the reason as it has been obtained by coercion and it could not be held to be useful and truthful and, therefore, no worth reliance.	Arguments of Petitioner
The panch witnesses could not be relied upon as they were not the natural witnesses i.e. resident of the said area.	Arguments of Petitioner
Thus, appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
51.	Arguments of Petitioner

Arguments of Respondent
Thus, no interference is called for.	Arguments of Respondent
We have considered the rival submissions made by learned Counsel for the parties and perused the record.	Reasoning
Evidence against the Appellant (A31) Confessional statement of Nasir Abdul Kader Kewal (A64) Confessional statement of Shahnawaz Khan (A128) Deposition of Usman Jan Khan (PW2) Deposition of Padmakar Krishna Bhonsle (PW62) Deposition of Ajit Surve (PW604) Confessional statement of Nasir Abdul Kader Kewal (A64)	None
In his confessional statement, he (A64) has revealed that he had been associated in smuggling activities and had been participating in landing and transportation alongwith coaccused.	Reasoning
He had earlier worked in Saudi Arabia and after coming back settled in Bombay.	Reasoning
His fatherinlaw Gulam Dastgir used to run the business of Matka at Bandra and thus, he (A64) also joined the said business.	Reasoning
Subsequently, he came in association of the smugglers and started helping them in landing and transportation.	Reasoning
He participated in landing and transportation from Shekhadi on 7.2.1993 and said that the associates of Tiger Memon (AA) were present including Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
At the instance of Tiger Memon, the bags of arms and explosives brought from the trawler were loaded in a tempo driven by Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
Confessional statement of Shahnawaz Khan (A128) Confessional statement of Shahnawaz Khan (A128) revealed that he participated in landing on 7.2.1993.	Reasoning
He (A128) alongwith coaccused brought the smuggled goods at Seashore and they found that two tempos were already parked there.	Reasoning
One tempo was being driven by Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
The smuggled goods were being loaded in both the tempos.	Reasoning
Tiger Memon (AA), Javed Chikna (AA) and Yeda Yakoob (AA) opened the sacks.	Reasoning
The accused (A128) saw that it contained rifles, handgrenades and bags containing black coloured powder in it.	Reasoning
Bullets were also there.	Reasoning
Subsequently, those tempos were unloaded at a building with a tower.	Reasoning
The goods were reloaded in the cavity of Commander jeeps.	Reasoning
The said vehicles (Jeeps) left for Bombay.	Reasoning
One tempo though empty followed the jeep.	Reasoning
Usman Jan Khan (PW2) identified the Appellant (A31) in the court.	Reasoning
Usman Jan Khan (PW2) deposed that Appellant (A31) participated in transportation of smuggled articles.	Reasoning
He deposed that on the relevant date they came out of the hotel after having the meal and noticed that Javed Chikna (AA) and Yeda Yakoob (AA), were standing near the white coloured tempo which was being driven by Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
Tiger Memon (AA) told Usman Jan Khan (PW2) to take a seat in the said tempo with the Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
He (PW2) sat in the tempo and they followed Tiger Memon (AA) and reached Shekhadi Coast at 9 p.m.	Reasoning
The smuggled goods had already arrived at the Coast.	Reasoning
The same were wrapped in gunny bags.	Reasoning
On the instruction of Tiger Memon (AA), the goods were loaded in the two tempos, one of them was being driven by Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
The goods were brought by the said tempo to Wangni Tower and were unloaded there.	Reasoning
It was at Wangni Tower that the goods were opened and the witness could see AK56 rifles, its rounds, handgrenades, pistols, magazines and RDX.	Reasoning
They were reloaded in the cavity of the jeeps parked there.	Reasoning
Padmakar Krishna Bhonsle (PW62) is the panch witness of the recovery of the vehicle and identified the vehicle recovered by Police Inspector, Anil Prabhakar Mahabole (PW506).	Reasoning
It was recovered at the disclosure statement of the Appellant (A31).	Reasoning
Ajit Shivram Surve (PW604), the Police Officer attached with DCB, CID who had been sent to get the samples prepared on 30.11.1993 by P.I. Shri Pharande, and he corroborated the incident of collection of samples as described by Asit Binod Ghorai (PW602).	Reasoning
He also named 3 persons who collected the samples as Kulkarni, Malve and Surve.	Reasoning
He (PW604) further deposed that he prepared the panchnama which was duly signed by the panch witnesses.	Reasoning
One application u s 457 of Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973 was filed by the Appellant (A31) before the Designated Court for release of the tempo and the same was allowed.	Reasoning
However, the court passed the order that the vehicle should be thoroughly examined by experts as to whether it contained any traces of the RDX.	Reasoning
It was in view thereof, 3 experts took samples on 30.11.1993 and the report dated 12.1.1994 detected traces of RDX.	Reasoning
This was corroborated by panch witness, Asit Binod Ghorai (PW602).	Reasoning
After conclusion of the trial, the learned Special Judge came to the conclusion that considering the confessions of the coaccused, Nasir (A64) and Shahnawaz (A128), as well as the statement made by Usman (PW2) there can be no conclusion other than the fact that the Appellant (A31) was involved in the Shekhadi landing and the transportation operation.	Reasoning
After going through the evidence on record i.e. the confession of the coaccused (A64 and A128) as well as the deposition of the various prosecution witnesses, we find no merit in this appeal.	Decision
Therefore, it is accordingly, dismissed.	Decision
Criminal Appeal No. 417 of 2011 This appeal has been preferred against the judgment and order dated 2.8.2007 passed by Special Judge of the Designated Court under the TADA for Bombay Blast Case No. 1 of 1993.	Issue
The charge against the Respondent (A31) had been framed mainly for conspiracy.	Issue
He was further charged with abetting and knowingly facilitating the commission of terrorist activities during the period of December, 1992 to April, 1993 by involving himself in landing and transportation of arms, ammunition and explosives smuggled into India by his coconspirators and the role played by the Respondent (A31) had been, transporting the said explosives landed at Shekhadi by his motor tempo No. MCU 4409 from Alibagh to Thane, which was unloaded in the godown of coaccused Liyakat Khan at MIDC.	Issue
After the trial, the said Respondent (A31) had been convicted for the main charge u s 3(3) TADA for transportation of the said explosives and awarded punishment of five years with a fine of Rs. 25,000/ but has been acquitted of the charge of conspiracy.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the Appellant has submitted that in addition to the general charge of conspiracy, the Respondent (A31) had been charged for assisting Tiger Memon (AA) and his associates in smuggling of arms, ammunition, handgrenades and explosives and its landing and transportation from Shekhadi on 7.2.1993.	Arguments of Petitioner
The Respondent was present at the instance of Tiger Memon and had transported the said contraband in his vehicle.	Arguments of Petitioner
Therefore, as the Respondent had been aware of the nature of the contraband, he cannot escape the liability of charge of conspiracy.	Arguments of Petitioner
Thus, the appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
65.	None
Mr. Mushtaq Ahmad, learned Counsel appearing for the Respondent (A31) has submitted that he is merely a transporter and not an associate of Tiger Memon, so he could not be involved in the charge of conspiracy.	Arguments of Respondent
Thus, the appeal is liable to be dismissed.	Arguments of Respondent
We have considered the rival submissions made by the learned Counsel for the parties and perused the evidence on record.	Reasoning
The learned Special Judge dealt with the issue and came to the conclusion	Reasoning
that in spite of the fact that the Respondent (A31) transported the contraband in his tempo and took the same to a far distance but there was nothing on record to show that he had knowledge of the kinds of goods transported in his tempo.	Reasoning
The parameters laid down by this Court in entertaining the appeal against the order of acquittal have to be applied.	Reasoning
From the evidence on record, it becomes clear that Yusuf Khan Kasam (A31) had participated in landing operation of contraband goods at Shekhadi as he was one of the persons who accompanied Tiger Memon and Ors.	Reasoning
and he had also been to Wangni Tower alongwith other associates and contraband material were loaded in tempo.	Reasoning
The tempo was taken by him alongwith absconding accused to Mumbra as instructed by Tiger Memon (AA).	Reasoning
This version is duly supported corroborated by Shah Nawaz Khan (A128) (Ex. 1569A) and by the evidence of Usman (PW2).	Reasoning
However, there is nothing on record to show that he was aware as of what kinds of contraband were being transported in his tempo.	Reasoning
We are of the considered opinion that no further interference is required and appeal lacks merit and is, accordingly, dismissed.	Decision

Reasoning

However, the tin boxes remained there.	Reasoning
After about a month, upon being asked by Shabbir, the said tin boxes and bags were taken to the first floor of the house of Ali Mian Faki, which was in close proximity to their house.	Reasoning
Janardhan Pandurang Gambas (A81) who is a resident of that area, and a fisherman who had participated in the smuggling with Shabbir, the Appellant (A30) and Abdulla Surati told the witness that the smuggled goods also contained weapons alongwith silver ingots.	Reasoning
Confessional Statement of Salim Kutta (A134)	Reasoning
He has corroborated the confessional statement of the Appellant (A30).	Reasoning
However, he (A134) did not say anything to show that the Appellant (A30) had knowledge regarding the contents of the boxes, particularly as regards the weapons.	Reasoning
He stated that the Appellant (A30) was present during the landing at Dighi Jetty and had made arrangements for labour and a boat.	Reasoning
After the loading of the landed goods on vehicles, the Appellant (A30) had also participated in negotiations with the police upon being interception by them.	Reasoning
Confessional Statement of Faki Ali Faki Ahmed (A74)	Reasoning
He (A74) corroborated the fact that the Appellant (A30) was a landing agent and a resident of Mhasla, and that the Appellant (A30) was well acquainted with Shabbir and Jamir (A133) who were involved in smuggling activities.	Reasoning
Deposition of Dilip Pansare (PW. 97)  	Reasoning
In his deposition, he reveals that he was a childhood friend of the Appellant (A30) and had been working in a government department.	Reasoning
However, he had driven the truck containing smuggled goods.	Reasoning
He has also deposed that upon interception by Inspector Patil (A116), a discussion ensued for half an hour, amongst the Appellant (A30), Gurav (A82), Shabbir Kadri and Inspector Patil (A116).	Reasoning
He identified the truck, though the owner and the driver of the truck were neither made accused, nor witnesses in this case.	Reasoning
Deposition of Vyankatesh Hirba (PW. 588)  	Reasoning
He was the officer who had effected the seizure of the Tempo bearing number MH065533 on 12.4.93.	Reasoning
The Appellant (A30) in his confession has stated that the said tempo was used to transport contraband items which were landed at Dighi Jetty.	Reasoning
Deposition of Dinesh Nakti (PW. 95) speaks of silver ingots and wooden boxes.	Reasoning
He identified the Appellant in court.	Reasoning
A conjoint reading of the confessional statement and deposition of witnesses reveal that Appellant (A30) had been an associate of Mohd.	Reasoning
Dossa (AA) and was aware of the fact that Mohd.	Reasoning
Dossa (AA) was involved in criminal activities.	Reasoning
A30 was also associate of coaccused Mechanic Chacha, Shabbir, Salim, Feroz and Jamir Sayyed Kadri.	Reasoning
A30 has been working as a landing agent for Mohd.	Reasoning
Dossa and Ors.	Reasoning
by arranging boats and coolies.	Reasoning
A30 was called to Mhasla on 4.12.1992 wherein he disclosed about the previous day landing to R.K. Singh (A102), Custom Officer.	Reasoning
Subsequent to the said meeting, A30 received Rs. 6.25 lacs from A136 and paid Rs. 1 lac to Mhasla Custom staff, Rs. 1.5 lacs to Shrivardhan Custom staff, Rs. 1.25 lac to R.K. Singh, Custom Officer.	Reasoning
A30 made arrangements for boats and coolies for another landing scheduled for 9.2.1993 and went to Dighi Jetty on his motorcycle on 9.2.1993.	Reasoning
He met Custom Inspector Gurav (A82) and started driving jeep of Gurav.	Reasoning
When the trucks carrying contraband smuggled goods were intercepted by police team headed by PSI V.K. Patil, A30 told Mr. Patil that the money for earlier landing had been paid to Mali Havaldar who was also member of that police team.	Reasoning
It was in his presence that Mechanic Chacha (A136) offered Rs. 10 lacs to PSI Patil and as they did not have money, they gave him 5 silver bricks as a security.	Reasoning
A30 alongwith others shifted 170 bricks in the cavity of two trucks of Bombay and 80 silver bricks were transferred from one truck to another at the residence of Shabbir (AA).	Reasoning
He also went to the residence of Shabbir.	Reasoning
A30 left the message at Shrivardhan Police Station that he would come with money on the night of 10.2.1993.	Reasoning
In his presence Rs. 2 lacs were paid to Patil, PSI at Shrivardhan Naka and Rs. 5 lacs were paid to Havaldars.	Reasoning
A30 received Rs. 3 lacs from Feroz on the same night to hand it over to Custom Officer of Alibagh and he handed it over to R.K. Singh and Sayyed.	Reasoning
After appreciating the evidence on record, the learned Designated Court reached the following conclusions However considering further events which had occurred at Ghonghar Patta i.e. interception of goods by police, allowing the same to be further proceeded after negotiations with smugglers, presence of A30 who was one of the main person, or effecting landing, his role in the said episode, it is difficult to perceive that at the said juncture A30 would not have gathered the knowledge of contraband material.	Reasoning
Needless to add that in cases of conspiracy it is difficult to except to have direct evidence and the inference about certain aspects is required to be drawn from established facts circumstances.	Reasoning
Thus having regard to all the aforesaid facets it is difficult to accept that at least at the said place A30 would not have gathered the nature of contraband goods also sent along with smugglers.	Reasoning
It is true that the said further acts committed by A30 being in the nature of continuing job for which he had agreed i.e. effecting the silver landing the same may not make him liable for being party to the conspiracy to commit the terrorist act or the larger conspiracy for which the charge at head 1stly is framed.	Reasoning
Such a conclusion is obvious as hardly there exists any other material denoting that alter completion of job of landing transportation, A30 having committed any act furthering object of ally conspiracy.	Reasoning
However, still now the further act being committed by A30 being with an expressed knowledge that the contraband material was containing arms ammunitions he will be squarely liable for commission of offence u s 3(3) of TADA.	Reasoning
Similarly the quantity of the said arms ammunition and the further acts actually committed by A30 would also make him liable for commission of offence u s 6 TADA.	Reasoning
Thus considering the said aspect it will be extremely difficult to accept that policemen would not have been aware about the nature of said goods in trucks which were in the said trucks i.e. silver and arms ammunitions as established by evidence.	None
Such an inference is inevitable as such evidence pertaining to landing clearly denotes of the material being of two different categories i.e. boxes and bachkies i.e. bundles.	Reasoning
Having regard to the same it is difficult to perceive that during inspection of trucks at least parcels from each category would not have been inspected by policemen.	Reasoning
Having regard to aforesaid even assuming that police party had permitted the said trucks to proceed away without inspection then also they cannot escape the liability arising out of said illegal omission committed by them.	None
In view of the same the knowledge of the nature of contraband goods will he required to be presumed for them.	None
Now considering the liability of A136 as revealed from the earlier discussion but without once again repeating the dilation made earlier it can be said that the same having revealed that A136 had become aware about the nature of goods after he was told regarding the same and the direction of accused Mustafa Dossa by A134.	None
As dilated earlier, it is clear that though A136 had continued with the said operation i.e. the operation of smuggling for which he had agreed earlier and in the process having committed the offence u s 3(3) of TADA still he cannot be said to be guilty for the offence of conspiracy to which A134 was said to be party.	None
Needless to add that considering the acts committed by A136, his liability remained confined to having committed the offence u s 3(3) and Section 6 of TADA.	None
The case of A30 also appears to be similar to that of A136 i.e. himself being not aware since the beginning of the goods to be smuggled being arms ammunitions and having acquired the knowledge about same during the midst of operation but in spite of the said knowledge having continued the said operation giving rise to the liability for commission of offence u s 3(3) Section 6 of TADA.	None
At the cost of repetition it will be required to be added that A136 and A30 having committed the relevant acts in the month of Jan., 1993 on the dates on which even Tiger Memon had not disclosed his intent regarding the places at which the explosions were to be committed in Bombay and themselves having not committed any act other than completing the smuggling operation for which they had agreed they cannot he held guilty for any offence of conspiracy though would he liable for commission of offences as stated aforesaid.	None
The confession of A134 does not show that the Appellant (A30) was aware of the contents of the contraband goods were arms and ammunition, either prior to the landing or thereafter.	None
However, considering the event of interception of the said goods by the police and the presence and involvement of the Appellant (A30) in negotiations with the police for clearing the said smuggled goods, it is difficult to believe, that at the said time, the Appellant (A30) did not have knowledge regarding the contraband material.	None
The confession of A133 however reveals that he (A133) had overheard the Appellant (A30) discussing the contents of the contraband after the items had landed.	None
In case of conspiracy, it is difficult to find direct evidence and thus, inference is required to be drawn from established facts and circumstances.	Reasoning
Hence, it is difficult to accept, that in the said circumstances the Appellant (A30) was unaware of the nature of the smuggled goods.	None
The acts that were further committed by the Appellant (A30), were in the nature of continuing his job in effecting the landing of silver, and the same may not make him liable as a party to the conspiracy to commit terrorist acts.	Reasoning
However, certain other acts that were committed by him (A30), with the express knowledge that the contraband material did in fact contain arms and ammunition, would make him liable for commission of offence u s 3(3) TADA.	Reasoning
Furthermore, the quantity of arms and ammunition, and the further acts committed by the Appellant would also make him liable for commission of offence u s 6 TADA.	None
The examination of the Appellant (A30) u s 313 of Code of Criminal Procedure reveals that he had assisted in the landing of goods at Dighi Jetty on 9.1.1993, and that he had taken the stand of being involved in the landings of silver even prior to the said event, and that he did not know the nature of the goods that were landed in the said landing.	None
The evidence of PWs 95 and 97 corroborates the fact that landing had occurred at the said time and place.	None
Their evidence reveals that the goods were packed in boxes, or in green coloured cloth bag that had been tied at the mouth, and that thus, they were not aware of the contents.	None
They had acted at the behest of the Appellant (A30) for which they had received payment.	None
The Appellant (A30) was aware that the smuggled goods were arms and ammunition, and even after acquiring such knowledge, he had continued the landing of the said smuggled goods.	None
This makes him liable for commission of offences u s 3(3) and 6 TADA.	None
His (A30) role is akin to that of Mechanic Chacha (A136).	None
Thus, we do not find any force in the appeal and it is accordingly dismissed.	Decision
The parameters laid down by this Court in entertaining the appeal against the order of acquittal have to be applied.	Reasoning
The evidence on record made it crystal clear that A30 was not only a close associate of Tiger Memon (AA) and acting as a landing agent, but a man of confidence who could negotiate with the police and customs officials to fix the amount of bribe for facilitating the smuggling and transportation of the smuggled contraband.	None
He was a person who had negotiated with the police at Gondghar Phata.	None
He had been fully aware of the nature of contraband, and in spite of coming to know that the contraband contained arms, ammunition and explosives, he continued to help the smugglers.	None
It is also evident that he had close association with Jaywant Keshav Gurav (A82), officer of the customs department, who had been helping the smugglers by taking a bribe through A30.	None
Therefore, in view of the above, we do not concur with the findings of fact recorded by the learned Designated Court that A30 could not be convicted for conspiracy.	None
In the facts and circumstances, the appeal filed by the Appellant (A30) is dismissed, and appeal filed by the State through CBI is allowed.	Decision
Appellant (A30) is convicted for the offence under Charge I, and awarded the life imprisonment.	Decision
The Designated Court is directed to take him into custody and send him to Jail to serve out the remaining sentence, if any.	Decision
Criminal Appeal No. 1420 of 2007 This appeal has been preferred against the judgment and order dated 31.5.2007, passed by Special Judge of the Designated Court under the TADA for Bombay Blast, Greater Bombay, by which the Appellant (A46) has been convicted u s 3(3) TADA.	Issue
However, Criminal Appeal No. 1031 of 2012 has been filed by the State against the said judgment as A46 stood acquitted of the charge of larger conspiracy.	Issue
Facts and circumstances giving rise to this appeal are that In addition to the main charge of conspiracy, the Appellant (A46) was charged for participation in landing and transportation of arms, ammunition, hand grenades and explosives like RDX to be used in Bombay blast on 12.3.1993.	Facts
He was further charged for abetting and participating in terrorist activities as the Appellant participated in the month of February 1993 alongwith coconspirators in the landing of arms, ammunition and explosives at Shekhadi.	Facts
Further he (A46) alongwith coaccused Asgar Mukadam drove other coaccused to Sahar Airport who had gone for training to Pakistan.	Facts
Further he delivered motor vehicle having arms, ammunition and explosives at the residence of Amjad Aziz Meharbux and thereby committed a separate offence punishable u s 3(3) TADA.	Facts
The Appellant was further charged u s 6 TADA for possessing and transporting arms, ammunition and explosives like hand grenades and detonators from Shekhadi to Bombay in an unauthorized manner.	Facts
The Appellant (A46) has been convicted u s 3(3) TADA, and given a sentence of R.I. 5 years and a fine of Rs.	Facts
25,000/ and in default of payment of fine, to suffer further imprisonment of six months u s 6 TADA, a sentence of R.I. 7 years and a fine of Rs.	Facts
50,000/ and in default of payment of fine, to further suffer one year RI.	Facts
Both the sentences were directed to run concurrently.	Facts
Hence, this appeal.	Facts
Shri Mushtaq Ahmad, learned Counsel appearing on behalf of the Appellant, has submitted that the Appellant (A46) has wrongly been enroped in the crime, he was no where involved.	Arguments of Petitioner
The confessional statements of the Appellant as well as the coaccused could not be relied upon as it has been obtained by coercion and it could not be held to be useful and truthful.	Arguments of Petitioner
More so, the learned Designated Court failed to appreciate the evidence in correct perspective.	Arguments of Petitioner
Therefore, appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
104.	Arguments of Petitioner
Per contra, Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing on behalf of the State, opposed the appeal contending that the Appellant had been deeply involved, not only in the landing and transportation of the smuggled goods, but being a very close associate of Tiger Memon (AA), was involved in the conspiracy also for which he has wrongly been acquitted.	Arguments of Respondent
The evidence on record fully established his involvement in the crime.	Arguments of Respondent
Thus, appeal lacks merit and is liable to be dismissed.	Decision
We have heard learned Counsel for the parties and perused the record.	None

The Designated Court after appreciating the entire evidence on record came to the conclusion that the Appellant (A46) actively participated in the Shekhadi landing and transportation operation of contraband goods i.e. AK56 rifles, ammunition, RDX etc. smuggled into the country by Tiger Memon (AA).	None
Confession of the Appellant (A46) revealed that he was in employment of the Memon family, however he had been to Shekhadi landing.	None
It could not be accepted that the Appellant (A46) was not aware of illegal business of Tiger Memon (AA) or about the nature of the contrabands smuggled into India.	None
The presence of the Appellant (A46) at the place where the goods were exchanged, at Wangni Tower and concealed into cavities of vehicles for transportation to Bombay, shows that he was a person of very close confident of Tiger Memon (AA).	None

In view of the above well reasoned judgment, we do not find any evidence on record to warrant interference in the conclusion drawn by the Designated Court.	Decision
The appeal lacks merit and is accordingly dismissed.	Decision
Criminal Appeal No. 1031 of 2012 This appeal has been filed by the State against the acquittal of the Respondent of the charge of larger conspiracy.	None
On the first charge, the learned Designated Court considered the entire evidence as referred to hereinabove and came to the conclusion However hardly there being cementing material for establishing nexus of A46 with conspiracy for which he is charged with and material having remained confined of himself having committed of aforesaid offences, it will be difficult to hold him liable for conspiracy for which he is charged with.	None
The same is obvious as hardly there is any material indicating A46 having committed any other acts because of which he is said to have further object of conspiracy for which he is charged with.	None
The parameters laid down by this Court in entertaining the appeal against the order of acquittal have to be applied.	Reasoning
In view of the above, the appeal is liable to be dismissed.	Arguments of Respondent
These appeals have been preferred against the judgments and orders dated 11th, 12th 16th, 17th October, 2006, 23rd/24th May, 2007 and 1st June, 2007 passed by the Special Judge of the Designated Court under the TADA in the Bombay Blast Case No. 1 of 1993, by which the Appellants have been convicted.	Issue
In view of the fact that each Appellant being assigned different acts, has been charged differently and has been awarded a different sentence, it is desirable to deal with the case of each Appellant separately to certain extent.	Issue
Suleman Mohammed Kasam Ghavte (A18)	None
The Appellant (A18) has been convicted on two counts u s 3(3) of TADA, and has been awarded 7 years rigorous imprisonment alongwith a fine of Rs. 25,000/ and in default of payment of fine, to suffer further RI for 6 months on each count.	Issue
In addition to the first charge of conspiracy, he has been charged for the following offences That he has participated alongwith the other conspirators in the landing and transportation of arms, ammunition and explosives at Shekhadi, and has smuggled goods into the country to be used for the commission of terrorist acts.	Issue
That, he alongwith coaccused Abdul Rehman Shaikh (A28) and Ors.	Issue
transported RDX explosives in Motor Tempo No. MMP4799 from Shekhadi to Panvel, and the same contained goods that were smuggled into the country by Tiger Memon (AA) and his associates for the purpose of committing terrorist activities.	Issue
That he participated in weapons training at Sandheri Borghat.	None
Thus, he has been charged u s 3(3) TADA and has been convicted and sentenced as mentioned hereinbefore.	Issue
Ms. Farhana Shah, learned Counsel appearing for the Appellant has submitted that the Appellant was unaware of the fact that the items being landed were those other than silver, and that he realized this fact only later.	Arguments of Petitioner

Thus, the appeal deserves to be allowed.	Arguments of Petitioner
125.	Arguments of Petitioner
On the contrary, Shri Mukul Gupta, learned Senior Counsel appearing for the State has submitted that the Appellant has been named by several witnesses of the Shekhadi landing.	Arguments of Respondent

We have heard learned Counsel for the parties and perused the record.	None
Evidence against the Appellant (A18) Confessional statement of Suleman Mohd.	None

In his confessional statement recorded on 18.4.1993 and 20.4.1993, the Appellant has disclosed that he had been working as a driver.	None
